60th- Humans in Space.webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/NASA_60th-_Humans_in_Space.webm/220px--NASA_60th-_Humans_in_Space.webm.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0a/NASA_60th-_Humans_in_Space.webm" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NASA_60th-_Humans_in_Space.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A documentary about spaceflight in NASA</div></div></div>\n<p>NASA has conducted many manned and unmanned spaceflight programs throughout its history. Unmanned programs launched the first American artificial <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellites</a> into Earth orbit for scientific and <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">communications</a> purposes, and sent scientific probes to explore the planets of the solar system, starting with <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, and including "<a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">grand tours</a>" of the outer planets. Manned programs sent the first Americans into <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> (LEO), won the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> by landing twelve men on the Moon from 1969 to 1972 in the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a>, developed a semi-reusable LEO <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a>, and developed LEO <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space station</a> capability by itself and with the cooperation of several other nations including post-Soviet <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. Some missions include both manned and unmanned aspects, such as the <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)"><i>Galileo</i> probe</a>, which was deployed by astronauts in Earth orbit before being sent unmanned to Jupiter.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manned_programs">Manned programs</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:S79-36529classof78.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3181" data-file-width="3976" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/78/S79-36529classof78.jpg/220px-S79-36529classof78.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/78/S79-36529classof78.jpg/330px-S79-36529classof78.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/78/S79-36529classof78.jpg/440px-S79-36529classof78.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:S79-36529classof78.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Some of NASA\'s first African-American astronauts including <a href="/wiki/Ronald_McNair" title="Ronald McNair">Dr. Ronald McNair</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guy_Bluford" title="Guy Bluford">Guy Bluford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_D._Gregory" title="Frederick D. Gregory">Fred Gregory</a> from the class of 1978 selection of astronauts.</div></div></div>\n<p>The experimental <a href="/wiki/Rocket-powered_aircraft" title="Rocket-powered aircraft">rocket-powered aircraft</a> programs started by NACA were extended by NASA as support for manned spaceflight. This was followed by a one-man <a href="/wiki/Space_capsule" title="Space capsule">space capsule</a> program, and in turn by a two-man capsule program. Reacting to loss of national prestige and <a href="/wiki/National_security" title="National security">security</a> fears caused by early leads in space exploration by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, in 1961 President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> proposed the ambitious goal "of landing a man on the Moon by the end of [the 1960s], and returning him safely to the Earth." This goal was met in 1969 by the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a>, and NASA planned even more ambitious activities leading to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manned_mission_to_Mars" title="Manned mission to Mars">manned mission to Mars</a>. However, reduction of the perceived threat and changing political priorities almost immediately caused the termination of most of these plans. NASA turned its attention to an Apollo-derived temporary space laboratory, and a semi-reusable Earth orbital shuttle. In the 1990s, funding was approved for NASA to develop a permanent Earth orbital <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space station</a> in cooperation with the international community, which now included the former rival, post-Soviet <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. To date, NASA has launched a total of 166 manned space missions on rockets, and thirteen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-15" title="X-15">X-15</a> rocket flights above the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">USAF</a> definition of spaceflight altitude, 260,000 feet (80&#160;km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="X-15_rocket_plane_.281959.E2.80.931968.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="X-15_rocket_plane_(1959&#8211;1968)">X-15 rocket plane (1959&#8211;1968)</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/North_American_X-15" title="North American X-15">North American X-15</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:X-15_in_flight.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1992" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/X-15_in_flight.jpg/220px-X-15_in_flight.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/X-15_in_flight.jpg/330px-X-15_in_flight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/X-15_in_flight.jpg/440px-X-15_in_flight.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:X-15_in_flight.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>X-15 in powered flight</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/North_American_X-15" title="North American X-15">X-15</a> was an NACA experimental rocket-powered <a href="/wiki/Hypersonic_speed" title="Hypersonic speed">hypersonic</a> research aircraft, developed in conjunction with the US Air Force and Navy. The design featured a slender fuselage with fairings along the side containing fuel and early computerized control systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AerospacewebX15_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-AerospacewebX15-30">[29]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RFP" title="RFP">Requests for proposal</a> were issued on December 30, 1954, for the airframe, and February 4, 1955, for the rocket engine. The airframe contract was awarded to <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">North American Aviation</a> in November 1955, and the XLR30 engine contract was awarded to <a href="/wiki/Reaction_Motors" title="Reaction Motors">Reaction Motors</a> in 1956, and three planes were built. The X-15 was <a href="/wiki/Drop_test" title="Drop test">drop-launched</a> from the wing of one of two NASA <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-52_Stratofortress" title="Boeing B-52 Stratofortress">Boeing B-52 Stratofortresses</a>, <i>NB52A</i> tail number 52-003, and <i>NB52B</i>, tail number 52-008 (known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Balls_8" title="Balls 8">Balls 8</a></i>). Release took place at an altitude of about 45,000 feet (14&#160;km) and a speed of about 500 miles per hour (805&#160;km/h).\n</p><p>Twelve pilots were selected for the program from the Air Force, Navy, and NACA (later NASA). A total of 199 flights were made between 1959 and 1968, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Flight_airspeed_record" title="Flight airspeed record">official world record</a> for the highest speed ever reached by a manned powered aircraft (current as of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=NASA&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>), and a maximum speed of Mach 6.72, 4,519 miles per hour (7,273&#160;km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fastest_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fastest-31">[30]</a></sup> The altitude record for X-15 was 354,200 feet (107.96&#160;km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAHyper_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAHyper-32">[31]</a></sup> Eight of the pilots were awarded Air Force <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronaut_Badge" title="Astronaut Badge">astronaut wings</a> for flying above 260,000 feet (80&#160;km), and two flights by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_A._Walker" title="Joseph A. Walker">Joseph A. Walker</a> exceeded 100 kilometers (330,000&#160;ft), qualifying as spaceflight according to the <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration_A%C3%A9ronautique_Internationale" title="F&#233;d&#233;ration A&#233;ronautique Internationale">International Aeronautical Federation</a>. The X-15 program employed mechanical techniques used in the later manned spaceflight programs, including <a href="/wiki/Reaction_control_system" title="Reaction control system">reaction control system</a> jets for controlling the orientation of a spacecraft, <a href="/wiki/Space_suit" title="Space suit">space suits</a>, and horizon definition for navigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAHyper_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASAHyper-32">[31]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">reentry</a> and landing data collected were valuable to NASA for designing the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AerospacewebX15_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-AerospacewebX15-30">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h4><span id="Project_Mercury_.281958.E2.80.931963.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Project_Mercury_(1958&#8211;1963)">Project Mercury (1958&#8211;1963)</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Project_Mercury" title="Project Mercury">Project Mercury</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glenn62.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1127" data-file-width="1528" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Glenn62.jpg/220px-Glenn62.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Glenn62.jpg/330px-Glenn62.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Glenn62.jpg/440px-Glenn62.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glenn62.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Glenn on Friendship 7: first US orbital flight, 1962</div></div></div>\n<p>Shortly after the Space Race began, an early objective was to get a person into Earth orbit as soon as possible, therefore the simplest spacecraft that could be launched by existing rockets was favored. The US Air Force\'s <a href="/wiki/Man_in_Space_Soonest" title="Man in Space Soonest">Man in Space Soonest</a> program considered many manned spacecraft designs, ranging from rocket planes like the X-15, to small ballistic <a href="/wiki/Space_capsule" title="Space capsule">space capsules</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Project1969_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Project1969-33">[32]</a></sup> By 1958, the space plane concepts were eliminated in favor of the ballistic capsule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When NASA was created that same year, the Air Force program was transferred to it and renamed <i>Project Mercury</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Mercury_Seven" title="Mercury Seven">first seven astronauts</a> were selected among candidates from the Navy, Air Force and Marine test pilot programs. On May 5, 1961, astronaut <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Alan Shepard</a> became the first American in space aboard <i><a href="/wiki/Mercury-Redstone_3" title="Mercury-Redstone 3">Freedom&#160;7</a></i>, launched by a <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Redstone_Launch_Vehicle" title="Mercury-Redstone Launch Vehicle">Redstone booster</a> on a 15-minute <a href="/wiki/Ballistics" title="Ballistics">ballistic</a> (suborbital) flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShepardsRide_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShepardsRide-35">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Glenn" title="John Glenn">John Glenn</a> became the first American to be launched into <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a>, by an <a href="/wiki/Atlas_LV-3B" title="Atlas LV-3B">Atlas launch vehicle</a> on February 20, 1962, aboard <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Atlas_6" title="Mercury-Atlas 6"><i>Friendship&#160;7</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AnAmericaninOrbit_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-AnAmericaninOrbit-36">[35]</a></sup> Glenn completed three orbits, after which three more orbital flights were made, culminating in <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Cooper" title="Gordon Cooper">L. Gordon Cooper</a>\'s 22-orbit flight <i><a href="/wiki/Mercury-Atlas_9" title="Mercury-Atlas 9">Faith 7</a></i>, May 15&#8211;16, 1963.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAManned_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAManned-37">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> (USSR) competed with its own single-pilot spacecraft, <a href="/wiki/Vostok_programme" title="Vostok programme">Vostok</a>. They sent the first man in space, by launching cosmonaut <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a> into a single Earth orbit aboard <a href="/wiki/Vostok_1" title="Vostok 1">Vostok 1</a> in April 1961, one month before Shepard\'s flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAGagarin_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAGagarin-38">[37]</a></sup> In August 1962, they achieved an almost four-day record flight with <a href="/wiki/Andriyan_Nikolayev" title="Andriyan Nikolayev">Andriyan Nikolayev</a> aboard <a href="/wiki/Vostok_3" title="Vostok 3">Vostok 3</a>, and also conducted a concurrent <a href="/wiki/Vostok_4" title="Vostok 4">Vostok 4</a> mission carrying <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Popovich" title="Pavel Popovich">Pavel Popovich</a>.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h4><span id="Project_Gemini_.281961.E2.80.931966.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Project_Gemini_(1961&#8211;1966)">Project Gemini (1961&#8211;1966)</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Project_Gemini" title="Project Gemini">Project Gemini</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3085" data-file-width="3802" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg/220px-Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg/330px-Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg/440px-Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ed_White_performs_first_U.S._spacewalk_-_GPN-2006-000025-crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ed_White_(astronaut)" title="Ed White (astronaut)">Ed White</a> on Gemini 4: first US <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spacewalk" title="Spacewalk">spacewalk</a>, 1965</div></div></div>\n<p>Based on studies to grow the Mercury spacecraft capabilities to long-duration flights, developing <a href="/wiki/Space_rendezvous" title="Space rendezvous">space rendezvous</a> techniques, and precision Earth landing, Project Gemini was started as a two-man program in 1962 to overcome the Soviets\' lead and to support the Apollo manned lunar landing program, adding <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_activity" title="Extravehicular activity">extravehicular activity</a> (EVA) and <a href="/wiki/Space_rendezvous" title="Space rendezvous">rendezvous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Docking_and_berthing_of_spacecraft" title="Docking and berthing of spacecraft">docking</a> to its objectives. The first manned Gemini flight, <a href="/wiki/Gemini_3" title="Gemini 3">Gemini 3</a>, was flown by <a href="/wiki/Gus_Grissom" title="Gus Grissom">Gus Grissom</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Young_(astronaut)" title="John Young (astronaut)">John Young</a> on March 23, 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheLastHurdle_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-TheLastHurdle-39">[38]</a></sup> Nine missions followed in 1965 and 1966, demonstrating an endurance mission of nearly fourteen days, rendezvous, docking, and practical EVA, and gathering medical data on the effects of weightlessness on humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlansforGemini3_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlansforGemini3-40">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AnAlternativeTarget_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-AnAlternativeTarget-41">[40]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Premier_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Premier of the Soviet Union">Soviet Premier</a> <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a>, the USSR competed with Gemini by converting their Vostok spacecraft into a two- or three-man <a href="/wiki/Voskhod_(spacecraft)" title="Voskhod (spacecraft)">Voskhod</a>. They succeeded in launching two manned flights before Gemini\'s first flight, achieving a three-cosmonaut flight in 1963 and the first EVA in 1964. After this, the program was canceled, and Gemini caught up while spacecraft designer <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Korolev" title="Sergei Korolev">Sergei Korolev</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz spacecraft</a>, their answer to Apollo.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Apollo_program_.281961.E2.80.931972.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Apollo_program_(1961&#8211;1972)">Apollo program (1961&#8211;1972)</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1714" data-file-width="2349" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg/220px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg/330px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg/440px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag-crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 11: Buzz Aldrin on the Moon, 1969.</div></div></div>\n<p>The U.S public\'s perception of the Soviet lead in the space race (by putting the first man into space) motivated President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> to ask the Congress on May 25, 1961, to commit the federal government to a program to land a man on the Moon by the end of the 1960s, which effectively launched the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Apollo was one of the most expensive American scientific programs ever. It cost more than $20 billion in 1960s dollars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butts_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Butts-43">[42]</a></sup> or an estimated $218&#160;billion in present-day US dollars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-44">[43]</a></sup> (In comparison, the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a> cost roughly $27.8&#160;billion, accounting for inflation.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harv_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-harv-45">[44]</a></sup> It used the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn_rocket" title="Saturn rocket">Saturn rockets</a> as launch vehicles, which were far bigger than the rockets built for previous projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AstroSat5_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-AstroSat5-46">[45]</a></sup> The spacecraft was also bigger; it had two main parts, the combined <a href="/wiki/Apollo_command_and_service_module" title="Apollo command and service module">command and service module</a> (CSM) and the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">Apollo Lunar Module</a> (LM). The LM was to be left on the Moon and only the command module (CM) containing the three astronauts would eventually return to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[note 2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The second manned mission, <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">Apollo 8</a>, brought astronauts for the first time in a flight around the Moon in December 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAApol8_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAApol8-48">[46]</a></sup> Shortly before, the Soviets had sent an unmanned spacecraft around the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siddiqi_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siddiqi-49">[47]</a></sup> On the next two missions docking maneuvers that were needed for the Moon landing were practiced<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NasaApollo9_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-NasaApollo9-50">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAApollo10_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAApollo10-51">[49]</a></sup> and then finally the Moon landing was made on the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a> mission in July 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NasaApollo11_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-NasaApollo11-52">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1856" data-file-width="2784" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg/220px-NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg/330px-NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg/440px-NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NASA_Apollo_17_Lunar_Roving_Vehicle-crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 17: <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Roving_Vehicle" title="Lunar Roving Vehicle">LRV</a>-003, 1972.</div></div></div>\n<p>The first <a href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_astronauts" title="List of Apollo astronauts">person to stand on the Moon</a> was <a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">Neil Armstrong</a>, who was followed 19 minutes later by <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Buzz Aldrin</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Michael_Collins_(astronaut)" title="Michael Collins (astronaut)">Michael Collins</a> orbited above. Five subsequent Apollo missions also landed astronauts on the Moon, the last in December 1972. Throughout these six Apollo spaceflights, twelve men walked on the Moon. These missions returned a wealth of scientific data and 381.7 kilograms (842&#160;lb) of lunar samples. Topics covered by experiments performed included <a href="/wiki/Soil_mechanics" title="Soil mechanics">soil mechanics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">meteoroids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">seismology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heat_transfer" title="Heat transfer">heat flow</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Laser_Ranging_Experiment" title="Lunar Laser Ranging Experiment">lunar ranging</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> The Moon landing marked the end of the space race; and as a gesture, Armstrong mentioned mankind when he stepped down on the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Apollo set major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_space_exploration_milestones,_1957%E2%80%931969" title="List of space exploration milestones, 1957&#8211;1969">milestones</a> in human spaceflight. It stands alone in sending manned missions beyond <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a>, and landing humans on another <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body" title="Celestial body">celestial body</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">Apollo 8</a> was the first manned spacecraft to orbit another celestial body, while <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">Apollo 17</a> marked the last moonwalk and the last manned mission beyond <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> to date. The program spurred advances in many areas of technology peripheral to rocketry and manned spaceflight, including <a href="/wiki/Avionics" title="Avionics">avionics</a>, telecommunications, and computers. Apollo sparked interest in many fields of engineering and left many physical facilities and machines developed for the program as landmarks. Many objects and artifacts from the program are on display at various locations throughout the world, notably at the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">Smithsonian\'s Air and Space Museums</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Skylab_.281965.E2.80.931979.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Skylab_(1965&#8211;1979)">Skylab (1965&#8211;1979)</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1998" data-file-width="2875" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg/220px-Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg/330px-Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg/440px-Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylab_and_Earth_Limb_-_GPN-2000-001055-crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skylab in 1974, seen from the departing <a href="/wiki/Skylab_4" title="Skylab 4">Skylab 4</a> CSM.</div></div></div>\n<p>Skylab was the United States\' first and only independently built <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space station</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skylabFirst_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-skylabFirst-56">[54]</a></sup> Conceived in 1965 as a workshop to be constructed in space from a spent <a href="/wiki/Saturn_IB" title="Saturn IB">Saturn IB</a> upper stage, the 169,950&#160;lb (77,088&#160;kg) station was constructed on Earth and launched on May 14, 1973, atop the first two stages of a <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a>, into a 235-nautical-mile (435&#160;km) orbit inclined at 50&#176; to the equator. Damaged during launch by the loss of its thermal protection and one electricity-generating solar panel, it was repaired to functionality by its first crew. It was occupied for a total of 171 days by 3 successive crews in 1973 and 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skylabFirst_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-skylabFirst-56">[54]</a></sup> It included a laboratory for studying the effects of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microgravity_environment" title="Microgravity environment">microgravity</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Telescope_Mount" title="Apollo Telescope Mount">solar observatory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skylabFirst_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-skylabFirst-56">[54]</a></sup> NASA planned to have a Space Shuttle dock with it, and elevate Skylab to a higher safe altitude, but the Shuttle was not ready for flight before Skylab\'s re-entry on July 11, 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livingandworking_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-livingandworking-57">[55]</a></sup>\n</p><p>To save cost, NASA used one of the Saturn V rockets originally earmarked for a canceled Apollo mission to launch the Skylab. Apollo spacecraft were used for transporting astronauts to and from the station. Three three-man crews stayed aboard the station for periods of 28, 59, and 84 days. Skylab\'s habitable volume was 11,290 cubic feet (320&#160;m<sup>3</sup>), which was 30.7 times bigger than that of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_Command_Module" title="Apollo Command Module">Apollo Command Module</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livingandworking_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-livingandworking-57">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Apollo.E2.80.93Soyuz_Test_Project_.281972.E2.80.931975.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Apollo&#8211;Soyuz_Test_Project_(1972&#8211;1975)">Apollo&#8211;Soyuz Test Project (1972&#8211;1975)</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Apollo%E2%80%93Soyuz_Test_Project" title="Apollo&#8211;Soyuz Test Project">Apollo&#8211;Soyuz Test Project</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="991" data-file-width="1498" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg/220px-Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg/330px-Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg/440px-Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_ASTP_crews_-_restoration-crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soviet and American crews with spacecraft model, 1975.</div></div></div>\n<p>On May 24, 1972, US President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_M._Nixon" title="Richard M. Nixon">Richard M. Nixon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> Premier <a href="/wiki/Alexei_Kosygin" title="Alexei Kosygin">Alexei Kosygin</a> signed an agreement calling for a joint manned space mission, and declaring intent for all future international manned spacecraft to be capable of docking with each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> This authorized the Apollo-Soyuz Test Project (ASTP), involving the rendezvous and docking in Earth orbit of a surplus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">Apollo Command/Service Module</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz</a> spacecraft. The mission took place in July 1975. This was the last US manned space flight until the first orbital flight of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> in April 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASTP_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASTP-59">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The mission included both joint and separate scientific experiments, and provided useful engineering experience for future joint US&#8211;Russian space flights, such as the Shuttle&#8211;Mir Program<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shuttle-MIR_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shuttle-MIR-60">[58]</a></sup> and the International Space Station.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Space_Shuttle_program_.281972.E2.80.932011.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_Shuttle_program_(1972&#8211;2011)">Space Shuttle program (1972&#8211;2011)</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_program" title="Space Shuttle program">Space Shuttle program</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shuttle-a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="806" data-file-width="602" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Shuttle-a.jpg/150px-Shuttle-a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Shuttle-a.jpg/225px-Shuttle-a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Shuttle-a.jpg/300px-Shuttle-a.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shuttle-a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Launch of a Space Shuttle.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_(7544385084).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1985" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_%287544385084%29.jpg/220px-Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_%287544385084%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_%287544385084%29.jpg/330px-Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_%287544385084%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_%287544385084%29.jpg/440px-Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_%287544385084%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Astronaut_Mae_Jemison_Working_in_Spacelab-J_(7544385084).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mae_Jemison" title="Mae Jemison">Mae Jemison</a> working in Spacelab in 1992. Spacelab was a major NASA collaboration with Europe\'s space agencies</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> became the major focus of NASA in the late 1970s and the 1980s. Planned as a frequently launchable and mostly reusable vehicle, four Space Shuttle orbiters were built by 1985. The first to launch, <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia" title="Space Shuttle Columbia"><i>Columbia</i></a>, did so on April 12, 1981,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Odyssey_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Odyssey-61">[59]</a></sup> the 20th anniversary of the first known human space flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Its major components were a <a href="/wiki/Spaceplane" title="Spaceplane">spaceplane</a> orbiter with an external fuel tank and two solid-fuel launch rockets at its side. The external tank, which was bigger than the spacecraft itself, was the only major component that was not reused. The shuttle could orbit in altitudes of 185&#8211;643&#160;km (115&#8211;400 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statute_mile" title="Statute mile">miles</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShuttleBas_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShuttleBas-63">[61]</a></sup> and carry a maximum payload (to low orbit) of 24,400&#160;kg (54,000&#160;lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AstroShutt_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-AstroShutt-64">[62]</a></sup> Missions could last from 5 to 17 days and crews could be from 2 to 8 astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShuttleBas_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-ShuttleBas-63">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 20 missions (1983&#8211;98) the Space Shuttle carried <a href="/wiki/Spacelab" title="Spacelab">Spacelab</a>, designed in cooperation with the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> (ESA). Spacelab was not designed for independent orbital flight, but remained in the Shuttle\'s cargo bay as the astronauts entered and left it through an <a href="/wiki/Airlock" title="Airlock">airlock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AstroSpacelab_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-AstroSpacelab-65">[63]</a></sup> Another famous series of missions were the <a href="/wiki/STS-31" title="STS-31">launch</a> and later <a href="/wiki/STS-61" title="STS-61">successful repair</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> in 1990 and 1993, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AstroHST_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-AstroHST-66">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1995, Russian-American interaction resumed with the <a href="/wiki/Shuttle%E2%80%93Mir_Program" title="Shuttle&#8211;Mir Program">Shuttle&#8211;Mir</a> missions (1995&#8211;1998). Once more an American vehicle docked with a Russian craft, this time a full-fledged space station. This cooperation has continued with Russia and the United States as two of the biggest partners in the largest space station built: the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS). The strength of their cooperation on this project was even more evident when NASA began relying on Russian launch vehicles to service the ISS during the two-year grounding of the shuttle fleet following the 2003 <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Columbia disaster">Space Shuttle <i>Columbia</i> disaster</a>.\n</p><p>The Shuttle fleet lost two orbiters and 14 astronauts in two disasters: <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster">Challenger</a></i> in 1986, and <i>Columbia</i> in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-delays1_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-delays1-67">[65]</a></sup> While the 1986 loss was mitigated by building the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour" title="Space Shuttle Endeavour">Space Shuttle <i>Endeavour</i></a> from replacement parts, NASA did not build another orbiter to replace the <a href="/wiki/STS-107" title="STS-107">second loss</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-delays1_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-delays1-67">[65]</a></sup> NASA\'s Space Shuttle program had 135 missions when the program ended with the successful landing of the Space Shuttle <i>Atlantis</i> at the Kennedy Space Center on July 21, 2011. The program spanned 30 years with over 300 astronauts sent into space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="International_Space_Station_.281993.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="International_Space_Station_(1993&#8211;present)">International Space Station (1993&#8211;present)</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ISS-assembly-animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="225" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/ISS-assembly-animation.gif/220px-ISS-assembly-animation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/ISS-assembly-animation.gif/330px-ISS-assembly-animation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fb/ISS-assembly-animation.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ISS-assembly-animation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of assembly of the ISS</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS) combines NASA\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Station_Freedom" title="Space Station Freedom">Space Station Freedom</a></i> project with the Soviet/Russian <i><a href="/wiki/Mir-2" title="Mir-2">Mir-2</a></i> station, the European <i><a href="/wiki/Columbus_(ISS_module)" title="Columbus (ISS module)">Columbus</a></i> station, and the Japanese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Experiment_Module" title="Japanese Experiment Module">Kib&#333;</a> laboratory module.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISSBook_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISSBook-69">[67]</a></sup> NASA originally planned in the 1980s to develop <i>Freedom</i> alone, but US budget constraints led to the merger of these projects into a single multi-national program in 1993, managed by NASA, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Federal_Space_Agency" title="Russian Federal Space Agency">Russian Federal Space Agency</a> (RKA), the <a href="/wiki/JAXA" title="JAXA">Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency</a> (JAXA), the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> (ESA), and the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Space_Agency" title="Canadian Space Agency">Canadian Space Agency</a> (CSA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PartStates_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-PartStates-70">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISSRG_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-71">[69]</a></sup> The station consists of pressurized modules, external <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Truss_Structure" title="Integrated Truss Structure">trusses</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_arrays" title="Solar arrays">solar arrays</a> and other components, which have been launched by Russian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_(rocket)" title="Proton (rocket)">Proton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(rocket_family)" title="Soyuz (rocket family)">Soyuz</a> rockets, and the US Space Shuttles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISSBook_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-ISSBook-69">[67]</a></sup> It is <a href="/wiki/Assembly_of_the_International_Space_Station" title="Assembly of the International Space Station">currently being assembled</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_Earth_Orbit" title="Low Earth Orbit">Low Earth Orbit</a>. The on-orbit assembly began in 1998, the completion of the <a href="/wiki/US_Orbital_Segment" title="US Orbital Segment">US Orbital Segment</a> occurred in 2011 and the completion of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orbital_Segment" title="Russian Orbital Segment">Russian Orbital Segment</a> is expected by 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The launch date of the modules have significantly slipped, and there is now some debate on whether they will ever launch. (May 2015)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> The ownership and use of the space station is established in intergovernmental treaties and agreements<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA-IGA_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA-IGA-74">[72]</a></sup> which divide the station into two areas and allow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Federation" title="Russian Federation">Russia</a> to retain full ownership of the Russian Orbital Segment (with the exception of Zarya),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSA-MOU_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-RSA-MOU-75">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> with the US Orbital Segment allocated between the other international partners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA-IGA_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-ESA-IGA-74">[72]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg/220px-STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg/330px-STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg/440px-STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS-135_final_flyaround_of_ISS_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The International Space Station as seen by the final STS mission</div></div></div>\n<p>Long duration missions to the ISS are referred to as <a href="/wiki/List_of_International_Space_Station_expeditions" title="List of International Space Station expeditions">ISS Expeditions</a>. Expedition crew members typically spend approximately six months on the ISS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> The initial expedition crew size was three, temporarily decreased to two following the Columbia disaster. Since May 2009, expedition crew size has been six crew members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> Crew size is expected to be increased to seven, the number the ISS was designed for, once the Commercial Crew Program becomes operational.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> The ISS has been continuously occupied for the past 18&#160;years and 108&#160;days, having exceeded the previous record held by <a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a>; and has been visited by astronauts and cosmonauts from <a href="/wiki/List_of_International_Space_Station_visitors" title="List of International Space Station visitors">15 different nations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10th_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-10th-80">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The station can be seen from the Earth with the naked eye and, as of 2019, is the largest artificial satellite in <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> orbit with a mass and volume greater than that of any previous space station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shuttlepresskit_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-shuttlepresskit-82">[80]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz</a> spacecraft delivers crew members, stays docked for their half-year-long missions and then returns them home. Several uncrewed cargo spacecraft service the ISS, they are the Russian <a href="/wiki/Progress_(spacecraft)" title="Progress (spacecraft)">Progress spacecraft</a> which has done so since 2000, the European <a href="/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle" title="Automated Transfer Vehicle">Automated Transfer Vehicle</a> (ATV) since 2008, the Japanese <a href="/wiki/H-II_Transfer_Vehicle" title="H-II Transfer Vehicle">H-II Transfer Vehicle</a> (HTV) since 2009, the American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dragon_spacecraft" title="Dragon spacecraft">Dragon spacecraft</a> since 2012, and the American <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_(spacecraft)" title="Cygnus (spacecraft)">Cygnus spacecraft</a> since 2013. The Space Shuttle, before its retirement, was also used for cargo transfer and would often switch out expedition crew members, although it did not have the capability to remain docked for the duration of their stay. Until another US manned spacecraft is ready, crew members will travel to and from the International Space Station exclusively aboard the Soyuz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> The highest number of people occupying the ISS has been thirteen; this occurred three times during the late Shuttle ISS assembly missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The ISS program is expected to continue until at least 2020, and may be extended beyond 2028.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span id="Commercial_programs_.282006.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_programs_(2006&#8211;present)">Commercial programs (2006&#8211;present)</span></h5>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Resupply_Services" title="Commercial Resupply Services">Commercial Resupply Services</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Crew_Development" title="Commercial Crew Development">Commercial Crew Development</a></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:338px;max-width:338px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="3402" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg/150px-COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg/225px-COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg/300px-COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Dragon being berthed to the ISS in May 2012</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cygnus_Orb-D1.8.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Cygnus_Orb-D1.8.jpg/180px-Cygnus_Orb-D1.8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Cygnus_Orb-D1.8.jpg/270px-Cygnus_Orb-D1.8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Cygnus_Orb-D1.8.jpg/360px-Cygnus_Orb-D1.8.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Cygnus berthed to the ISS in September 2013</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>The development of the Commercial Resupply Services (CRS) vehicles began in 2006 with the purpose of creating American commercially operated uncrewed cargo vehicles to service the ISS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> The development of these vehicles was under a fixed price milestone-based program, meaning that each company that received a funded award had a list of milestones with a dollar value attached to them that they didn\'t receive until after they had successfully completed the milestone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasaRendezvous2010-2_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasaRendezvous2010-2-87">[85]</a></sup> Companies were also required to raise an unspecified amount of private investment for their proposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On December 23, 2008, NASA awarded Commercial Resupply Services contracts to <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orbital_Sciences_Corporation" title="Orbital Sciences Corporation">Orbital Sciences Corporation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> SpaceX uses its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falcon_9_rocket" title="Falcon 9 rocket">Falcon 9 rocket</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dragon_spacecraft" title="Dragon spacecraft">Dragon spacecraft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> Orbital Sciences uses its <a href="/wiki/Antares_(rocket)" title="Antares (rocket)">Antares</a> rocket and <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_(spacecraft)" title="Cygnus (spacecraft)">Cygnus spacecraft</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Dragon_C2%2B" title="Dragon C2+">first Dragon resupply mission</a> occurred in May 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cygnus_1" title="Cygnus 1">first Cygnus resupply mission</a> occurred in September 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> The CRS program now provides for all America\'s ISS cargo needs; with the exception of a few vehicle-specific payloads that are delivered on the European <a href="/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle" title="Automated Transfer Vehicle">ATV</a> and the Japanese <a href="/wiki/H-II_Transfer_Vehicle" title="H-II Transfer Vehicle">HTV</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:328px;max-width:328px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SpaceX_Dragon_v2_Pad_Abort_Vehicle_(16661791299).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/SpaceX_Dragon_v2_Pad_Abort_Vehicle_%2816661791299%29.jpg/160px-SpaceX_Dragon_v2_Pad_Abort_Vehicle_%2816661791299%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/SpaceX_Dragon_v2_Pad_Abort_Vehicle_%2816661791299%29.jpg/240px-SpaceX_Dragon_v2_Pad_Abort_Vehicle_%2816661791299%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/SpaceX_Dragon_v2_Pad_Abort_Vehicle_%2816661791299%29.jpg/320px-SpaceX_Dragon_v2_Pad_Abort_Vehicle_%2816661791299%29.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Dragon V2</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CST-100.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2250" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/CST-100.jpg/160px-CST-100.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/CST-100.jpg/240px-CST-100.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/CST-100.jpg/320px-CST-100.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Rendering of CST-100 in orbit</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Crew_Development" title="Commercial Crew Development">Commercial Crew Development</a> (CCDev) program was started in 2010 with the purpose of creating American commercially operated crewed spacecraft capable of delivering at least four crew members to the ISS, staying docked for 180 days and then returning them back to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berger_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berger-94">[92]</a></sup> It is hoped that these vehicles could also transport non-NASA customers to private space stations such those planned by <a href="/wiki/Bigelow_Aerospace" title="Bigelow Aerospace">Bigelow Aerospace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup> Like COTS, CCDev is also a fixed price milestone-based developmental program that requires some private investment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasaRendezvous2010-2_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasaRendezvous2010-2-87">[85]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2010, NASA announced the winners of the first phase of the program, a total of $50&#160;million was divided among five American companies to foster research and development into human spaceflight concepts and technologies in the private sector. In 2011, the winners of the second phase of the program were announced, $270&#160;million was divided among four companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft20110418_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-ft20110418-96">[94]</a></sup> In 2012, the winners of the third phase of the program were announced, NASA provided $1.1 billion divided among three companies to further develop their crew transportation systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-August2012_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-August2012-97">[95]</a></sup> In 2014, the winners of the final round were announced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCtCapBlogAnnounce_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-CCtCapBlogAnnounce-98">[96]</a></sup> SpaceX\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dragon_V2" title="Dragon V2">Dragon V2</a> (planned to be launched on a <a href="/wiki/Falcon_9_v1.1" title="Falcon 9 v1.1">Falcon 9 v1.1</a>) received a contract valued up to $2.6 billion and Boeing\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CST-100" title="CST-100">CST-100</a> (to be launched on an <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a>) received a contract valued up to $4.2 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20140921_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn20140921-99">[97]</a></sup>  NASA expects these vehicles to begin transporting humans to the ISS in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Beyond_Low_Earth_Orbit_program_.282010.E2.80.932017.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Beyond_Low_Earth_Orbit_program_(2010&#8211;2017)">Beyond Low Earth Orbit program (2010&#8211;2017)</span></h4>\n<p>For missions beyond <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> (BLEO), NASA has been directed to develop the <a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">Space Launch System</a> (SLS), a Saturn-V class rocket, and the two to six person, beyond low Earth orbit spacecraft, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orion_spacecraft" title="Orion spacecraft">Orion</a>. In February 2010, President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>\'s administration proposed eliminating public funds for the <a href="/wiki/Constellation_program" title="Constellation program">Constellation program</a> and shifting greater responsibility of servicing the ISS to private companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Achenbach_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Achenbach-101">[99]</a></sup> During a speech at the Kennedy Space Center on April 15, 2010, Obama proposed a new heavy-lift vehicle (HLV) to replace the formerly planned <a href="/wiki/Ares_V" title="Ares V">Ares V</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-speech_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-speech-102">[100]</a></sup> In his speech, Obama called for a manned mission to an asteroid as soon as 2025, and a manned mission to Mars orbit by the mid-2030s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-speech_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-speech-102">[100]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/NASA_Authorization_Act_of_2010" title="NASA Authorization Act of 2010">NASA Authorization Act of 2010</a> was passed by Congress and signed into law on October 11, 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa2010_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa2010-103">[101]</a></sup> The act officially canceled the Constellation program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa2010_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa2010-103">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Authorization Act required a newly designed HLV be chosen within 90 days of its passing; the launch vehicle was given the name "Space Launch System". The new law also required the construction of a beyond low earth orbit spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orion_spacecraft" title="Orion spacecraft">Orion spacecraft</a>, which was being developed as part of the Constellation program, was chosen to fulfill this role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-authorizationact_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-authorizationact-105">[103]</a></sup> The Space Launch System is planned to launch both Orion and other necessary hardware for missions beyond low Earth orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup> The SLS is to be upgraded over time with more powerful versions. The initial capability of SLS is required to be able to lift 70 mt into <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">LEO</a>. It is then planned to be upgraded to 105 mt and then eventually to 130 mt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-authorizationact_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-authorizationact-105">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schedule_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-schedule-107">[105]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploration_Flight_Test_1" title="Exploration Flight Test 1">Exploration Flight Test 1</a> (EFT-1), an unmanned test flight of Orion\'s crew module, was launched on December 5, 2014, atop a <a href="/wiki/Delta_IV_Heavy" title="Delta IV Heavy">Delta IV Heavy</a> rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schedule_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-schedule-107">[105]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploration_Mission_1" title="Exploration Mission 1">Exploration Mission-1</a> (EM-1) is the unmanned initial launch of SLS that would also send Orion on a <a href="/wiki/Circumlunar_trajectory" title="Circumlunar trajectory">circumlunar trajectory</a>, which is planned for 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schedule_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-schedule-107">[105]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars2020Rover-JourneyToMars-Humans-Rover-20140731.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="577" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Mars2020Rover-JourneyToMars-Humans-Rover-20140731.jpg/440px-Mars2020Rover-JourneyToMars-Humans-Rover-20140731.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Mars2020Rover-JourneyToMars-Humans-Rover-20140731.jpg/660px-Mars2020Rover-JourneyToMars-Humans-Rover-20140731.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Mars2020Rover-JourneyToMars-Humans-Rover-20140731.jpg/880px-Mars2020Rover-JourneyToMars-Humans-Rover-20140731.jpg 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mars2020Rover-JourneyToMars-Humans-Rover-20140731.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>NASA Graphic for the Journey to Mars</div></div></div>\n<p>NASA\'s next major space initiative is to be the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Orbital_Platform-Gateway" title="Lunar Orbital Platform-Gateway">Lunar Orbital Platform-Gateway</a> (LOP-G, formerly known as the "Deep Space Gateway").  This initiative is to involve the construction of a new "Space-Station" type of habitation, which will have many features in common with the current <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>, except that it will be in orbit about the Moon, instead of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup>  This space station will be designed primarily for non-continuous human habitation.  The first tentative steps of returning to manned lunar missions will be <a href="/wiki/Exploration_Mission-2" title="Exploration Mission-2">Exploration Mission-2</a> (EM-2), which is to include the Orion crew module, propelled by the SLS, and is to launch in 2022.  This mission is to be a 10- to 14-day mission planned to briefly place a crew of four into <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit" title="Lunar orbit">Lunar orbit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schedule_107-3"><a href="#cite_note-schedule-107">[105]</a></sup> The construction of the "Lunar Orbital Platform" is to begin with the following <a href="/wiki/Exploration_Mission-3" title="Exploration Mission-3">Exploration Mission-3</a> (EM-3), which is planned to deliver a crew of four to <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit" title="Lunar orbit">Lunar orbit</a> along with the first module(s) of the new space-station.  This mission will last for up to 26 days.\n</p><p>On June 5, 2016, NASA and DARPA announced plans to also build a series of new X-planes over the next 10 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Building_X_Planes_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Building_X_Planes-109">[107]</a></sup> One of the planes will be the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quiet_Supersonic_Technology" title="Quiet Supersonic Technology">Quiet Supersonic Technology</a> project, burning low-carbon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofuels" title="Biofuels">biofuels</a> and generating quiet sonic booms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Building_X_Planes_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Building_X_Planes-109">[107]</a></sup>\n</p><p>NASA plans to build full scale deep space habitats such as the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Orbital_Platform-Gateway" title="Lunar Orbital Platform-Gateway">Lunar Orbital Platform</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nautilus-X" title="Nautilus-X">Nautilus-X</a> as part of its <a href="/wiki/Next_Space_Technologies_for_Exploration_Partnerships" title="Next Space Technologies for Exploration Partnerships">Next Space Technologies for Exploration Partnerships</a> (NextSTEP) program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2017, NASA was directed by the congressional NASA Transition Authorization Act of 2017 to get humans to Mars-orbit (or to the Martian surface) by 2033.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-futurism.com_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-futurism.com-111">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unmanned_programs">Unmanned programs</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unmanned_NASA_missions" title="Unmanned NASA missions">Unmanned NASA missions</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer-3-4.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Pioneer-3-4.gif/170px-Pioneer-3-4.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Pioneer-3-4.gif/255px-Pioneer-3-4.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/Pioneer-3-4.gif 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer-3-4.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_3" title="Pioneer 3">Pioneer 3</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_4" title="Pioneer 4"><i>4</i></a> launched in 1958 and 1959, respectively</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JWST_Full_Mirror.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1560" data-file-width="1041" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/JWST_Full_Mirror.jpg/220px-JWST_Full_Mirror.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/JWST_Full_Mirror.jpg/330px-JWST_Full_Mirror.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/JWST_Full_Mirror.jpg/440px-JWST_Full_Mirror.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JWST_Full_Mirror.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>JWST main mirror assembled, November 2016</div></div></div>\n<p>More than 1,000 unmanned missions have been designed to explore the Earth and the solar system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAHisStat_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAHisStat-113">[111]</a></sup> Besides exploration, communication satellites have also been launched by NASA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAHis6_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAHis6-114">[112]</a></sup> The missions have been launched directly from Earth or from orbiting space shuttles, which could either deploy the satellite itself, or with a rocket stage to take it farther.\n</p><p>The first US unmanned satellite was <a href="/wiki/Explorer_1" title="Explorer 1">Explorer 1</a>, which started as an ABMA/JPL project during the early part of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a>. It was launched in January 1958, two months after Sputnik. At the creation of NASA, the Explorer project was transferred to the agency and still continues to this day. Its missions have been focusing on the Earth and the Sun, measuring magnetic fields and the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, among other aspects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAExp_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAExp-115">[113]</a></sup> A more recent Earth mission, not related to the Explorer program, was the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a>, which was brought into orbit in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_Solar_System" title="Inner Solar System">inner Solar System</a> has been made the goal of at least four unmanned programs. The first was <a href="/wiki/Mariner_program" title="Mariner program">Mariner</a> in the 1960s and 1970s, which made multiple visits to <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and one to <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>. Probes launched under the Mariner program were also the first to make a planetary flyby (<a href="/wiki/Mariner_2" title="Mariner 2">Mariner 2</a>), to take the first pictures from another planet (<a href="/wiki/Mariner_4" title="Mariner 4">Mariner 4</a>), the first planetary orbiter (<a href="/wiki/Mariner_9" title="Mariner 9">Mariner 9</a>), and the first to make a <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a> maneuver (<i><a href="/wiki/Mariner_10" title="Mariner 10">Mariner 10</a></i>). This is a technique where the satellite takes advantage of the gravity and velocity of planets to reach its destination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPL_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPL-117">[115]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first successful landing on Mars was made by <i><a href="/wiki/Viking_1" title="Viking 1">Viking 1</a></i> in 1976. Twenty years later a rover was landed on Mars by <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_Pathfinder" title="Mars Pathfinder">Mars Pathfinder</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlanetSocMars_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlanetSocMars-118">[116]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_4" style="width:220px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_5" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/NASA_60th-_What%E2%80%99s_Out_There.webm/854px--NASA_60th-_What%E2%80%99s_Out_There.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="237.771" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="NASA_60th-_What&#8217;s_Out_There.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/49/NASA_60th-_What%E2%80%99s_Out_There.webm/NASA_60th-_What%E2%80%99s_Out_There.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm;
